General Raige's son Kitai learns this as he goes to retrievethe second emergency beacon in the tail end of the crashed ship averting many dangers and barely surviving some. The ship is approximately 2 days away. Kitai's father is struggling to stay alive with 2 badly broken legs, one with arterial damage. 6305307261|4.0|Nosferatu: The Vampire<br /><br />I just got Werner Herzog's Nosferatu: The Vampyre (Nosferatu: Phantom der Nacht). I'm 30 min's in & I can tell you this is a marvelous Dracula movie. It is faithful to the source material (except for the closing scene)but with Herzog at the controls & color available (though little used) it is the visual embodiment of the story of Dracula. Herzog is the world's best Documentarian & you wouldn't think that would lead to great fiction. In his case it does. He directed Aguirre: The Wrath of God & Fitzcarraldo along with many others including, Signs of Life. It's images, images, images conceived in such a way as to perfectly portray his subject matter & give rise in his viewer to what is going on in that character mentally & emotionally. No one is his equal at this. Back to the movie which anyone would not believe a man whose fame grew from documentaries would be capable of in a horror story. This is Halloween the holiday in a disc folks.<br /><br />Finished it & I must say what an achievement. There is not one scene of violence in the whole movie unless you call Jonathan Harker nicking his finger at the dinner table violence. The ending scene strangely does not remotely follow the book & I will dock it a full point. 1/2 for not following the book & 1/2 because to me, the ending scene is the weakest part of the story. Dracula seems to be completely unaware of when crosses (supposed to cause Dracula great pain) are in the scene. Drac carries his coffin through a graveyard of crosses with no reaction then, when he puts his coffin down he turns & sees 2 that are 1/10 the size & cowers. When Drac visits Lucy in her bedroom she is wearing a silver cross (extremely potent) but Drac has no reaction again. I don't understand or forgive that. It is the first mistake of that magnitude I've seen in any of his films.<br /><br />I can just hear the world's blood & gore horror crowd complaining about this atmospheric & apprehensive Dracula. The opening scene is perhaps the finest in the Horror genre. There is no blood at all to speak of. There is no crescendo after crescendo of horror. What there is is an oppressive & brooding sense of evil that is eminent, which never goes away, but carries throughout the common scenes as well as the uncommon encounters. I loved it. 8.5 out of 10 stars. Did I mention I was PO'ed about the crosses? Except for that though, the scene construction is superbly Herzog. I loved his use of music too.<br /><br />XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX<br /><br />My wife came home early to say, &#34;Let's watch Nosferatu with Klaus. I allowed my arm to be twisted. I also came up with another dissatisfaction. It's the character of Van Helsing. I understand they were intent on showing Dracula as a sympathetic character & to have a monomaniacal foe of all that's holy would be counterproductive. The milk-toast they make of him though is just wrong. This one is not the Dr. that rides in as help in a time of trouble. He is rather the epitome of the country Dr. Many have done this story with no Van Helsing at all & I think that would have been better. Keep the character that they used but don't call him Van Helsing. I'll deduct another 1/2 point for that & make it 8 out of 10 stars after a second viewing. As my wife said after seeing it, &#34;Aguirre was 10 out of 10. This one isn't, yet the imagery is as good & the brooding evil never leaves.&#34;<br /><br />Nosferatu: The Vampyre (English version)<br /><br />On my disc it isn't cut at all. Not even one scene. The English version comes from a dirtier source print. Snow is bad at the beginning & slowly fades for about 20-25 min's then it is gone. I didn't notice a whole lot of camera angle changes but what was evident was an overall use of slightly less light. You can really see that in the backlighting of Count Dracula as he walks down the corridor to Jonathon Harker'sroom. It is noticeable throughout however. It steals the sheen off of fabrics causing it to appear less contrast was used. The ending doesn't change at all.<br /><br />My wife pointed out & I agree that Shadow of the Vampire is better done. Max Schreck in that is as eerie & imposing with more latitude given for intensity. Willem Defoe against Klaus Kinski is a &#34;can't lose&#34; match on maniacal expression & I'll see both of these many times over the years. B00EBJ624O|5.0|It makes perfect sense to create a musical partnership between Yuja Wang and Gustavo Dudamel, both superstars with DG contracts.  With the added influence of the Simon Bolivar Orchestra of Venezuela, there's a great feeling of freshness and youthful spontaneity.  For all the thrill of excitement that these young musicians bring, some will worry that they'll show signs of inexperience.  Despite flashy careers and great popularity, some critics charge them of shallowness, and lack of intrinsic musicality.  Is there good cause for premonition?<br /><br />Starting off with the Rachmaninov 3rd Concerto, long a virtuoso display piece, we hear musicianship that is well-considered and very mature.  Rachmaninov readings of late have tended to shy away from gooey sentimentality in favor of more refinement and modernist sensibility.  Here the first two movements could almost be seen as dry-eyed, yet they move with flexibility and true involvement.  Every phrase sounds natural and there's dramatic undercurrent that lends a feeling of expectation.  Wang has an inspiring technique, so she tackles every challenge with no sign of stress.  But she doesn't simply run through the music either; she has commanding control and one feels she gives everything she has.  She chooses the shorter, easier cadenza in the first movement, something some listeners will bewail, but it's a minor cavil either way.  In the finale we get to hear actual fireworks for the first time, with both Wang and Dudamel letting go with frenzied abandon.  I can't imagine anyone listening without being captivated by the sheer charisma of these musicians--it's quite an experience.<br /><br />If anything the Prokofiev 2nd Concerto is better and there's comparatively little competition.  With its percussive, modernist idiom, it hardly hurts when interpreters take it with towering precision and unbounded energy.  It's a thrilling display of pyrotechnics here.  Nothing sounds brusque though, and it's a good thing that Wang can be biting and sarcastic without banging out the notes.  There's freedom in the phrasing actually, which makes the piece playful without wearing on the listener's ear.  Dudamel is vivacious and makes the most out of the rip-roaring orchestral part--the orchestra plays with surprising accuracy.  In all, the force of two fiery young musicians makes this concerto a romp from beginning to end.<br /><br />There's a chance this recording will be a divider, since there's a unique combination of refinement and dynamic involvement that some will find self-conscious.  I can only argue that charisma wins out in the end.g can be biting and sarcastic without banging out the notes. 1593275080|5.0|The rising popularity of LEGO as an art form is emphatically punctuated in Mike Doyle's book, Beautiful LEGO. Whereas the title seems to obviously declare what's inside, once you crack the pages and take a look, you'll wonder how Doyle kept his title so simple.  This book is spectacular and should be more adequately titled, \\"267 pages of stunning, crisp, high resolution, full color photos of LEGO creations that will blow you away.\\"  Even the back cover and fold-over dust jacket are packed with artfully arranged high res. images, rather than busy words.  This book is definitely destined to become the de rigueur of coffee table LEGO books [and may shove a few modern art books out of the way as well.]<br /><br />Beautiful LEGO takes you on a jaw-dropping photo journey of the creations of around 70 different LEGO artists from around the world, including brief interviews with about a dozen of them.  The LEGO creations depicted in the book range in size from Doyle's 200,000 piece, Contact-- the fantasy city featured on the cover-- down to 10 piece birds and snails with amazing character.  Unlike other LEGO books where the photography and resolution are secondary, every image in this book appears to consist of a well-lit, clutter-free, high res. images destined for the Smithsonian.  Many images are so clear you can see the airtight seams between even the smallest of bricks.<br /><br />Among the strengths of this book is that Doyle does not linger on any particular LEGO artist, building style or motif for more than a few pages.  The upside to this is that the reader gets a wide-ranging view of the amazing capabilities of the artist and their bricks, but the downside is that you may crave more.  Although the outcome is positive, the images flow like a mad-mans brainstorm (which is some of the appeal of this book).  You never know what you will see next.  Creations include space stations, fish, steampunk-style attic treasures, birds, cars, dragons, dissected frogs, fast food,cartoon characters,  modern homes...you name it.<br /><br />It should be clarified that this book is not a building instruction book, nor a how-to book. It does overflow, however, with creativity and beauty that will prove inspiring beyond any other LEGO book to date. Observant LEGO fans will certainly be able to create some of the smaller works should they desire, but most of the works are placed in this book to enjoy.

B00BMKH5NW|5.0|I can't think of any words powerful enough to adequately express how much I loved Fangirl, but I'm going to spend the rest of this review trying to find them.<br /><br />Rainbow Rowell's second young adult novel follows reticent Cath through her freshman year of college as she copes with social anxiety, a suddenly distant twin sister, balancing her fanfiction writing career with her Fiction Writing class, boys who smile too much, and boys who lie too much.  There's more too - child abandonment, mental instability, alcohol abuse, romance.  It comes together to form one of the best contemporary novels I've ever read.  Seriously.  It's up there with my favorite of favorites, Anna and the French Kiss.  Possibly higher.<br /><br />I think this is primarily because of how natural everything felt.  Even the heavier topics I mentioned above manage to fit organically into the plot without ever overtaking the story. It's not a novel about alcohol abuse or family problems or anxiety.  It's about Cath.  All those issues just happen to be part of her life.<br /><br />That naturalness applies to the characters as well.  No matter how sparsely or frequently they appear, every single one of them seems just as real as anyone I've met during my own first year of college; as if (to use a phrase from the book) they were about to \\"evolve right off the page.\\"  That goes for everyone from Cath's twin sister Wren, to her snarky roommate Reagan, to Levi the ever-friendly Starbucks employee who always seems to be hanging around their room.<br /><br />The relationships between all these characters, too, were gorgeously built.  They grew slowly and honestly.  No snap friendships or instant romance to be found here.  Everything took time (sometimes, given Cath's trust issues, quite a bit of time).<br /><br />This is usually where I might add in a however or but; where I might comment on something that bugged me or didn't exactly work.  Hard as I search my mind, though, I can't think of one negative thing to say about Fangirl.  Not one.<br /><br />So, instead, I'll talk about something that could have gone badly, but didn't.  Really, really didn't.<br /><br />Much of the story centers around a made-up, Harry Potter-esque book series that Cath has not only adored since childhood, but for which she's spent the last two years penning a novel-length fanfiction.  I was skeptical at first of how Rowell would handle the fact that her main character was devoting a good chunk of her thoughts to books and characters with whom her readers would be unfamiliar.<br /><br />It wasn't a problem.  Namely, because she made sure readers would be familiar with the Simon Snow world-within-a-world she'd created.  Throughout the novel, Rowell separates sections with excerpts from both the Simon Snow books themselves and Cath's Simon Snow fanfiction.  This too somehow seemed perfectly natural and necessary; partly because these segments often had something to do with what was happening in Cath's life, and partly because they provided proof of Cath's passion for writing.  Rowell doesn't simply say that her main character is a writer.  She shows her to be one.  And, just as importantly, Rowell gives her readers the opportunity to care about Simon Snow, and so to understand why Cath does.  (For the record, I miss Simon and Baz right now just as much as I do Cath and Levi.)<br /><br />On that note, I happen to be a mild (by which I mean obsessed) fanfiction addict, but I hope I haven't given the impression that you have to be a fangirl yourself to enjoy this book.  You don't.  Fandom culture is certainly an element of the story, but, like the alcohol and abandonment plot lines,  it never overpowers the narrative.<br /><br />I'm still not sure I managed to express how much I enjoyed this book, but I'll leave at this: Read Fangirl.  It's well written, quirky, deep, and definitely worth both the time and bookshelf space.

0618918248|5.0|I bought this book on kindle on amazon.co.uk so my buying it might not show on this site.<br /><br />This book has, I believe, attained the status of 'iconic' and a 'must-read'. It is well worthy of that status. It is an excellent widely-encompassing look at the falsity of so-called 'religion'. I write 'so-called' because what passes for religion seems to me to be vulgar blatant superstition.<br /><br />Dawkins looks at 'religion' from many viewpoints and he gives a good description of the various 'proofs' for religion that have been put forward by believers over the centuries. He despatches them all very easily, which is to be expected since each one contains at least one glaring fallacy. The two that are probably most influential today are the 'First Cause' and 'Pascal's Wager'. I think many people seem to fail to see that the 'First Cause' has a fallacy that literally jumps out at a reader. This is that if everything needs a cause for it to exist then why should God be any different. By the logic presented, God needs a cause. Also, as Dawkins point out, an intelligent designer would need to be more complex than his creation. So God's existence needs even more explanation than the universe does.<br /><br />Pascal's Wager is the idea that, since the pains of hell are so great and the reward of heaven so attractive, it makes sense to adopt religious belief 'just in case' it turns out to be true. The idea is insulting, of course, and of no merit as Dawkins points out. Actually, at time of writing, on YouTube there is an excellent short video on 'Pascal's Wager' by Jaclyn Glenn at<br />[...]<br /><br />There is much of interest in this book. I found the parts on 'cargo cults' and on 'memes' most interesting. There is also an extensive bibilography.<br /><br />I recommend this book highly.

B00FFVLYTU|5.0|I think mine will be something like the 462nd review so I wonder what I can say that hasn't already been said and done so in the most amazing ways.  Still, I will go ahead and add my stars to the tab this remarkable series is racking up.  I can't sleep and need something to do.<br /><br />From the opening of the first episode I was taken in the grip of this drama.  It had me by the throat and punched me in the gut.  I literally couldn't breathe in some parts.  Be warned, most of the time it is so painful to watch.  I cried.  I couldn't help it.  I got caught up in it.  As a parent, I had no problem whatsoever relating to the nightmare unfolding in the lives of the family of the murdered boy. Talk about intense! I started watching as soon as the first episode became available via Amazon's instant streaming but I actually didn't watch each episode as soon as it was downloaded because I didn't know if I could take any more!  But I did continue to watch.  I had to...a saga about each individual in the show was being revealed.  It became more than the effect this death had on the victim's family.  The impact and trauma expanded to every single person in the small community of Broadchurch.<br /><br />Don't expect any relief or distraction from the horror of the murder of a young boy.  It was sad without any let up.  The music was beautiful and haunting adding to and intensifying the melancholy.  There was no waking up from the nightmare and the sun coming up in the morning.  Which is exactly what I imagine it would be like to live through such a shattering event. There is no happy ending.  The final episode, 8, is a devastating reveal of the murderer and the added devastation it causes. Someone murdered this child in a small close-knit community.  Everyone in Broadchurch is thinking, &#34;It must have been a stranger that did this evil thing, right?&#34;  I kept thinking, could finding out that the murderer is someone known to this traumatized family make it even more painful? Perhaps. Still, the people of Broadchurch comforted themselves with the knowledge that no one they know could do such a horrific thing and as a beach front &#34;tourist&#34; town, the murderer has to be a stranger.  And then...suspicion begins to be cast about...upon locals...we have a front row seat to see what this does...who begins pointing fingers at whom.  No one is left untouched and unaffected by the fallout.  Broadchurch is ripped apart...and so was I.<br /><br />I loved the writing.  I liked the way the story was told/revealed bit by bit.  I appreciated that I wasn't &#34;let in&#34; on who the killer was or even what motivated the murder until the very end.  I got the impression that a philosophy was being put forth.  For me, the &#34;lesson&#34; was one of not jumping to conclusions, that appearances can definitely be deceiving; things aren't usually as straight forward as they first appear, are not black and white.  Another thing I enjoyed was how the writing gave us realistic and believable characters doing and saying things that fit their situation.  Yes, at first glance we are only given enough information to tempt us to jump to conclusions but ultimately things made sense.  Peoples' actions and behavior always has a why behind them and the writing did a good job of realistically portraying the why...when it's revealed, that is.  You know, someone says something and we wonder, &#34;Why&#34; did they say that?  A lot of the time the dialogue in both film and books, for me, is hugely disconnected between what is said and why a person would possibly say it.  Would they really say that?  It really bugs me when this disconnect happens and Broadchurch had very little of it.<br /><br />I liked having to unravel the layers of the folks of Broadchurch, myself, rather than having it done for me.  I enjoyed how &#34;the why&#34; which motivated and shaped each person's behavior was revealed.  How and why did they get where they were in their lives?  Some sins do indeed have long shadows and they don't necessarily have to be our own sins.  So, how do people live through, get beyond, and even overcome events in their lives?  There are a lot of walking wounded.  And they abound in this program. Some programs are pure escapism, not this one. It's a drama, a tragedy. It is not a typical crime procedural (I believe that is the term.) I didn't think that this show was mainly about solving the murder.  It was mainly about how the murder effected the family and then in a ripple effect every other person in the community and further.<br /><br />I was transported from my sofa into the community of Broadchurch.  I became a neighbor and emotionally connected.  By developing the characters over several episodes there was a greater depth to everyone.<br /><br />I can't find fault with any of the acting. It was completely top-notch.  I am a huge fan of British programming and I am constantly awed by the degree of greatness in the acting!  These people are good.  They don't get their roles because they look good on camera in jeans without a T-shirt!  I've never followed the Dr. Who series so am not familiar with Tennant in that roll but I thought he was masterful.  He played a mournful and deeply scarred person to perfection.  He was driven in his pursuit of the truth.  I wanted to know what had happened to him that had shaped him. He was an outsider in Broadchurch. He was sent there to solve this murder yet, there was a connection and it was personal, that was made plain, but it wasn't a connection to the victim or anyone involved.  So, what was it?<br /><br />Now, on the other hand, Detective Ellie (Olivia Colman) was a local.  Her son was a close friend of the murdered boy.  Her family was closely connected to and lived just a few doors down from the victim.  Like everyone else in Broadchurch this was up-close-and-personal. She should have been in charge of the case but was blindsided by having Tennant assigned instead.  To add insult to injury she was forced to endure his rough and insensitive manner toward everyone she had known forever. The suspects were her friends and she began the investigation pretty much unable to even consider that someone she knows could have committed such an atrocity to one of their own. As the story unfolds she begins to see people once so familiar to her in different ways than she did before.  She discovers she didn't really know them at all.  Colman was brilliant.  The emotion she brought to the role was astounding.  She was completely believable and real to me.<br /><br />It would be a glaring omission not to mention how well all the other roles were acted.  The young murdered boy was painfully beautiful.  His parents portrayed the crippling and devastating blow of losing their son absolutely superbly. You'll see the supporting characters perform their parts perfectly.<br /><br />At the conclusion we are notified that &#34;Broadchurch will return.&#34;  For me, I will watch to see what the writer(s) will bring to the screen next.  I would like to see what happens to these folks.  Could I stand to see another 8 episodes of such heart wrenching melancholy? I'm really not sure.  This offering was painful for me.  It was so real. So, in follow-up programming I would like to see something with the intense acting, superb writing but perhaps a little relief from the drama.  I don't need comedy.  I'm talking some breathing room.  A person needs to be able to breathe when a program is nearly 8 hours long.  Anyway, I'll be taking a peek at what might be happening next in Broadchurch.<br /><br />The rating is appropriate for the content of the subject matter.  There were no scenes of graphic sex or gory violence, (thank goodness!), which is a welcome change of what one normally sees these days in any kind of drama involving murder.  No autopsies or mutilated corpse was depicted.  (The subject matter was horrific enough without adding any of that!) The only sex scene wasof the couple after the act and both were either clothed or completely covered. All of these things are something I greatly appreciate because I don't want to see it.  There is mild swearing that I've come to expect in a program of this rating and I think only one use of the &#34;F&#34; word at the end.ner toward everyone she had known forever. B002UBRFDC|4.0|This book is a very good book! It is not only a genuine goddamn page-turner, but it gives the reader an authentic-feeling look at a rich, swashbuckling time period and a glimpse into something of the Japanese cultural mind. It's a tightly drawn but sprawling story about love, politics, people stabbing themselves in the belly, prostitution, more stabbing, and falconry. It is a wholly different world from our own, with foreign characters and perspectives, yet totally human and relatable -- you know, like a goddamn novel. Moreover, Clavell, whom I tend to think of as a masculine writer, draws incredibly complex and powerful female characters in a time and place that ostensibly oppresses females. Unlike most, I never felt that Korn have \\"lost their way\\" instead appreciating each album for what they were, rather than what I thought they should be. Their previous album, \\"The Path of Totality,\\" made it really hard for me to appreciate because it lost one of the most important things that made Korn what they are, their instruments. In that album, Jonathan's vocals were great, but you couldn't tell the difference between an actual instrument and a computer-generated sound from time to time. Many fans were worried that future albums would have the same problem, the \\"Metalstep\\" sound. Thankfully, that's gone.<br /><br />Other reviews have accused this new album of some of the same problems, of including Metalstep (Dubstep-infused Metal) like the previous album, but that is definitely not the case. Some of the Metalstep elements of the past album is still present, but only in the form of special effects used only when necessary. The guitars lead this album, and it features some of the best work Korn has ever done.<br /><br />When guitarist Head was announced to return, many assumed that it would mark the return of Korn's '90's style. Anyone that knows this band, knows that they refuse to follow any preconceived formula. Instead of a clone of Self Titled, or Life is Peachy, we get a brand new Korn sound that they've never done before. This album features a brand new, somewhat darker (and sometimes creepy) sound infused with elements from past albums for safe measure. Although their style has been reinvented, it still manages to give a listening experience similar to their Untouchables album, which is deep and takes a few listens to \\"get it.\\" That said, I loved the album from the beginning, but I love it even more now that I've heard it over ten times.<br /><br />The lead single from the album (Never Never) had fans worried, because that song sounds likesomething that could have been on \\"The Path of Totality\\", heavily influenced by Dubstep. In fact, in Never Never, you can barely notice the guitars at all. The single was massively hated and became an instant black eye for the band, somewhat of a laughing stock. Due to that single, many have written off this album before it was released. But let me assure you, although I don't hate the song like other people do, Never Never is easily the worst song on the album. In fact, the second single (Love & Meth) isn't ground breaking either. The rest of the album though, is astonishing. It's extremely surprising how good this album is, it has the right mix of both the old and new styles, forming a new style that is quite possibly their best yet. The instrumentation, vocals, everything is great!<br /><br />Highlights for me include \\"Lullaby for a Sadist,\\" \\"Victimized,\\" \\"It's All Wrong,\\" and \\"Mass Hysteria.\\"<br /><br />The one thing I can complain about is the Autorip feature of this album. I preordered the Deluxe CD/DVD edition, and I got an email this morning that the Autorip version has been added to my Cloud Player, but was disappointing to find out that it's the standard edition, NOT the deluxe edition, so I'll still have to rip the disc myself. What's the point of offering Autorip, if the MP3 edition you're given is not the same one you've purchased?

B000P0J0G0|5.0|How do you summarize a show like this one? Maybe you don't. For better or worse, some reviewers have compared it to Monty Python. The verdict's still out on that, unless it is a reference to the random formatting of the show through the years.<br /><br />They lampooned, they parodied, they attacked...but they usually did it without ruffling a hair out of place, and they only seldom swore compared to some of the pure stand-up figures of history. The few moments they spent on screaming fits or foul language are mostly so well-timed that they just made the big picture even funnier. There is something intriguing about a duo that can do such busy things without losing composure or having a fit of camera shyness even once. It seems that the ridiculousness of their comedy style was invented for their own amusement, and all the better if some people laughed on the right part of the joke. Some people have complained that the show feels dated now. Well, yes, that's becoming true with each passing year, but these sketches are still heaps less topical than many other shows of this era. Many of the skits are based so much on \\"verbal frotting\\" (to quote Fry) that this factor is quite forgivable.<br /><br />The writing is typically very smart - it's certainly not exclusionary in the MENSA fashion, but viewers who excel at public debating, writing, or simply read their brains out will really enjoy the subtleties. The way the scripts are delivered is proof positive of just how smart Fry & Laurie are (perhaps a little too smart, since it was later revealed that both of them have dealt with severe mood problems). At the same time, the \\"vox pops\\" and occasional fits of completely random lines or sketches - Tony Inchpractice for one -  save the show from becoming snobbish. The on-stage music you find in some episodes is not variety show music but rather a parody or \\"silly song,\\" and even these portions are amusing in some way (Hugh Laurie really shines in these moments of fusing his music and comedy skills together).<br /><br />I will admit that the show is a mixed bag in some ways. I hardly laugh at all for the \\"Tony & Control\\" bits, but love so many other characters (check out the unapologetic take on \\"Oprah\\") that I choose to simply skip over the less funny parts instead of rip them apart in an Amazon review. I'm not saying that these actors or their show is infallible, but I *am* saying that it's not worth bashing the entire series just for a few less-than-golden moments.<br /><br />As a fun bonus, season 2's disc also has the lengthy \\"Cambridge Footlights Revue\\" episode that Fry and Laurie did together in the early 1980s (look for the excellent tribute to \\"Dracula\\").

I'm very curios to see how they play it out in the alternate ending!<br /><br />---<br /><br />I just finished binge-watching Breaking Bad and I have to say this show is perhaps the finest show I have ever seen. All credit goes to Vince Gilligan for his brilliant writing and narration, and the outstanding cast that has portrayed the characters so well.<br /><br />If you think about it, the show is fairly low key in terms of its canvas and sets (the other end of this spectrum would be the opulent Game of Thrones where HBO has spared no expense - I'm a big fan of that show as well). The strength of Breaking Bad is its simplistic sets, gripping and powerful pace and narration, and brilliant acting. These factors are what make the show an absolute delight to watch.<br /><br />NO SPOILERS really - The story is about a simple Albuquerque-based Chemistry professor, Walter White, who has perhaps had subdued resentment all his life as his colleagues have passed him by to more success and money than he was able to achieve. He was Mr. Nice Guy who avoided conflict, played by the book, loved his family, and was very much the guy next door. In part, he was satisfied with his simple life at home and at work, but subconsciously he always knew he was capable of much more and felt the world unfairly passed him by. Diagnosed with stage three lung cancer, he figures he has little to lose, and wants to make sure he provides for his family before his departure. This desperation triggers a whirlwind of events as he goes on to become a master chef of the best quality meth in the southwest, perhaps the entire country.<br /><br />The narration beautifully walks through the changing phases and personalities of Walt. From wanting to cook meth because he wants to leave enough money behind for his family, to eventually doing so because he was having fun doing it, Walt goes through various shades of transformation. Towards the darkest parts of season 6, you almost feel like the guy has completely lost it. Yet, strangely you will find yourself rooting for Walt, I guess partly because his nice family guy image stays with you throughout (I mean he leaves his house every morning to cook meth carrying a lunch bag with his name written on it!). The various battles in his mind, at his home, at work, are all very well acted out and directed.<br /><br />Kudos to Bryan Cranston for playing Walter White so brilliantly, he just owns this role. I cannot think of any other actor who could have portrayed Walt so well. Aaron Paul, playing Jesse - Walt's tormented partner in crime (for the most part), is pure Genius with a capital G. If you want to truly see his brilliant acting, check out two scenes in particular - one is I think in season 4, when he has a nervous breakdown in front of Walt after finding out he has to accompany Gus to Mexico, and the second is when he's basically having a one-sided conversation at dinner with Skyler and Walt. Again, pure Genius! My other favorite characters are Hank Schrader, played brilliantly by Dean Norris (he has a slight Jack Nicholson thing going...I think), and the sleazy but brilliant lawyer Saul Goodman played by Bob Odenkirk (they actually have a site bettercallsaul.com - check it out to see how sleazy he comes across). B00D2ZF3GQ|1.0|A sci-fi adventure void of excitement and personality, After Earth takes an interesting idea and turns it into a meandering, plodding drip of a movie.<br /><br />M. Night Shyamalan once made great films. The Sixth Sense, Unbreakable and Signs were unique genre movies that benefited from Shyamalan's creative approach to story and direction. Several turkeys later and the once critic's darling has become a gun for hire, with After Earth his latest gig. That his once popular name does not appear on any of the promo material indicates the dire professional straits Shyamalan has found himself in.<br /><br />The names that are front and centre belong to Will Smith and his son Jaden. They (of course) play father and son General Cypher Riage and Kitai Raige, respectively, inhabitants of a distant planet colonised by humans, who find themselves in a race for survival after a mid-space journey leads to a crash landing on an Earth that has evolved into a much dangerous place a millennium after mankind left it. Being a Shyamlan film of course there is more plot featured, yet such exposition was dizzying enough in the film so no need to repeat it here.<br /><br />A case of \\"too much info\\" is the least of the problems which plague After Earth, a bland sci-fi adventure that literally begins with a crash and never recovers. With a meandering script (courtesy of Shyamalan and Gary Wittia) as its guide, After Earth plodders from one scenario to another as hot-headed Kitai journeys through treacherous land to find an emergency beacon, while his heroic yet emotionally stunted father is sidelined with a gruesome injury that could be fatal. Despite working with two tense filled scenarios, Shyamalan fails to make either effectively engrossing in any way.<br /><br />A big problem is the performances, with the Smith duo unable to make these characters, their scenario or their relationship worth investing in. Jaden Smith is just too green to pull of such a demanding lead role, with Kitai the engine that should have made After Earth run smoothly instead of spluttering along like the lemon it is. In turn Kitai becomes an intensely unlikeable character who cannot get the audience on side.<br /><br />Just as bad is big \\"Willie\\" Smith, who delivers a flaccid performance bereft of that charisma that benefits most of his movies, replaced here with a clenched jaw intensity that becomes stale, fast. It is ironic that a film about a son following the legacy of his father has seen the Smith family shoot themselves in the foot in their second outing as a collective (matriarch Jada Pinkett Smith joins her husband as a producer).<br /><br />Worst is the body blow given to Shyamalan's career with yet another stinker. There is still talent there to be sure with some moments of tension creeping through After Earth now and again, yet a new approach is needed by Shyamalan to recapture former glories.<br /><br />The first step should be to align himself with a screenwriter - perhaps a Damon Lindelof (Star Trek) or Jonatahon Nolan (The Dark Knight Rises) - who can take his million dollar ideas and shape it into something worth watching. The second? Stay away from vain family projects that are sure to leave egg on your face.<br /><br />[...]engine that should have made After Earth run smoothly instead of spluttering along like the lemon it is. 0553296981|5.0|Enter Anne Frank's brave new world  -- the world of Kitty, Peter, Anne's favorite pen, dancing and ballet, and yes, even the German SS, and final betrayal.  It's been with us now nearly 70 years, immortalized in diary notes found scattered on the floor of her family's hiding place in Amsterdam after their arrest. A source of healing for nearly four generations since its publication in 1947, \\"The Diary of Anne Frank\\" records the coming of age of a gifted precocious teenager of 14 becoming a young woman,  fiercely honest and lucid and aware nearly all the time and therefore able to vividly recreate on diary page after delightful diary page her celebration of the joy of living.<br /><br />70 years with us? So what's so new about it? What point could this reviewer possibly be making that hasn't been made before -- and perhaps even  better?  This: To the growing number of men and women denying the Holocaust ever happened,  \\"The Diary\\" is a stark and poignant reminder that it did happen, that Anne, her sister Margot, and their mother were three of its millions of victims.<br /><br />Every generation must revisit the world of Anne Frank on its own terms, with its own vision. Being more or less the fourth generation after Anne, it's our turn:  In a parallel universe half a globe away from her world, in the Bronx, NY, more or less 16 years before Anne began recording her diary entries,  a 4-year old boy runs upstairs to his mother cooking  in the kitchen, looks up into her face asking: \\"What am I?\\" Years later, the boy a brilliant retired engineer  by now recalled: \\" Mother said that I was a good boy [and continued working].  I repeated the question and she replied that I was a very good boy.\\"<br /><br />\\"My next query was - Am I Italian?  My mother then said - So that is what this is all about.  No, you are not Italian.  You are a Jew.\\"<br /><br />\\"With that response, I ran down the stairs and with a loud voice I echoed \\"I am a Jew\\", \\"I am a Jew\\" all the way to the building's front steps and onto the street, where many neighborhood kids were gathered.  One of the older boys, who may have been six or seven years old said - in an equally loud voice - OK Jew - get the ball - you're holding up the game.\\"<br /><br />Anne Frank's \\"Diary\\" and Saul's \\"Vignettes\\", experienced half a globe apart, parallel and complete each other. Same generation. Anne died in the Belsen concentration camp, on the eve of being liberated. Saul grew up and fought in World War II trying to liberate her and others.<br /><br />For the rest of this remarkable connection read \\"The Diary\\", then download and read a Kindle edition of \\"Vignettes: The Story of My Life\\" by Saul Frechtel at http://www.amazon.com/s/ref=nb_sb_noss?url=search-alias%3Daps&field-keywords=VIGNETTES+saul+frechtel.'s front steps and onto the street, where many neighborhood kids were gathered. B00000DHTF|5.0|As one of the most prolific artists of the last 40 years, Bruce Springsteen has released some of the most beloved rock classics ever. However, beyond his impressive officially released catalog lies a world of undiscovered gems that never saw the light due to either poor judgment or simply not fitting the artistic vision The Boss was going for at the time. Some of these became B-sides and some were even performed live without official release, but many simply remained lost to the sands of time until the 1998 boxed set \\"Tracks.\\"<br /><br />\\"Tracks\\" barely scrapes the surface as far as the many great previously unreleased Springsteen recordings go, but it does a perfectly credible job of collecting some of the best, beginning with his four song audition for John Hammond at Columbia Records in 1972.<br /><br />The first disc covers 1972-1978. Not every song is a winner, and some are particularly terrible (\\"Bishop Danced,\\" \\"Zero and Blind Terry\\"), but songs \\"Seaside Bar Song,\\" \\"Rendezvous,\\" \\"Bring on the Night,\\" \\"Hearts of Stone,\\" and \\"Don't Look Back\\" are as good as anything he ever put on an official album and stack up well against the rest of his catalog.<br /><br />Disc 2 covers 1979-82 and is easily the best of the four, covering \\"The River\\" through the early \\"Born in the U.S.A.\\" sessions. Much has been made of how badly Bruce dropped the ball when selecting what went onto \\"The River,\\" and this disc really hammers that home: letting great pop songs like \\"Roulette,\\" \\"Where the Bands Are,\\" \\"Loose Ends,\\" \\"Take `Em as They Come,\\" and \\"Be True\\" go in favor of lesser fare like \\"Two Hearts,\\" \\"Crush on You,\\" \\"I'm a Rocker\\" truly hurt the record in terms of quality. Other treats here include an alternate \\"Stolen Car\\" (not as sparse or as haunting as the released take but effective in its own right) and a chilling early cut of \\"Born in the U.S.A.\\" that drives the actual meaning of the lyrics home more effectively than the anthemic (and great in its own right) album version. In addition to the wealth of truly great songs on here, the cd itself is the best sounding release of Springsteen's career; no other cd, studio or live, sounds as crisp and clear and leaps out of the speakers like a wild jackal.<br /><br />Disc 3 contains great cuts like \\"My Love Will Not Let You Down,\\" the thrilling \\"Stand On It,\\" and the popular \\"Pink Cadillac,\\" but loses steam towards the end with forgettable fare like \\"When You Need Me,\\" \\"Two for the Road,\\" and \\"The Honeymooners,\\" though it does contain the touching Mother's Day ballad \\"The Wish.\\"<br /><br />Disc 4 gets some heat from fans because it draw from the period where he disbanded the E Street Band, but the majority of the material is surprisingly strong. \\"Seven Angels\\" is a terrific rocker, while \\"Sad Eyes\\" is a tender ballad with a moving, sympathetic vocal from Bruce. \\"Loose Change\\" is one of his most haunting songs, while \\"Part Man, Part Monkey\\" is dumb but very funny. \\"Happy\\" is in the running for the best love song he ever wrote. The two best moments come from the two cuts at the end, the achingly soulful \\"Back in Your Arms\\" and the haunting \\"Brothers Under the Bridge,\\" one of Bruce's quintessential Vietnam ballads.<br /><br />\\"Tracks\\" isn't perfect (a follow-up is most certainly needed), but is a solid sampling of some of Bruce's best songs that got awayn addition to the wealth of truly great songs on here, the cd itself is the best sounding release of Springsteen's career; no other cd, studio or live, sounds as crisp and clear and leaps out of the speakers like a wild jackal.<br /><br />Disc 3 contains great cuts like \\"My Love Will Not Let You Down,\\" the thrilling \\"Stand On It,\\" and the popular \\"Pink Cadillac,\\" but loses steam towards the end with forgettable fare like \\"When You Need Me,\\" \\"Two for the Road,\\" and \\"The Honeymooners,\\" though it does contain the touching Mother's Day ballad \\"The Wish.\\"<br /><br />Disc 4 gets some heat from fans because it draw from the period where he disbanded the E Street Band, but the majority of the material is surprisingly strong. \\"Seven Angels\\" is a terrific rocker, while \\"Sad Eyes\\" is a tender ballad with a moving, sympathetic vocal from Bruce. \\"Loose Change\\" is one of his most haunting songs, while \\"Part Man, Part Monkey\\" is dumb but very funny. \\"Happy\\" is in the running for the best love song he ever wrote. 0988178532|5.0|Reading Brandt Legg's Outview is a fast paced, rollicking good time.  Although the prose is often thoughtful and the descriptions of the Oregon coast evocative, the story moves at an exhilaratingly furious clip.<br /><br />Nate, the protagonist, is a humble, self-deprecating teen who at first seems to be suffering from horrible nightmares and hallucinations.  He is an average kid battled by demons we the readers do not understand.  As we get to know Nate better, as well as the obstacles that beset him, Nate's unassuming nature becomes important as he picks up numerous psychic and new age powers.  Even as he sky climbs, astral travels and walks through portals he remains a character with whom the reader can identify and route for, as opposed to an inscrutable superhero.<br /><br />Outview takes readers places they may have not been before, or even imagined.  Past lives are revealed, along with the concept of forgiveness for past life transgressions.  Sacred groves are discovered as well as portals between both time and space.  Important thought is given to the nature of the soul itself, as a separate entity from the corporal body.  Mystics also appear throughout the book, providing guidance and knowledge for the main character as he struggles to put meaning to the events swirling around him.<br /><br />Based on the age of the majority of the main characters Outview seems to be a book aimed at the teenage market.  As such, it does an admirable job of introducing some fascinating new age concepts in an unthreatening way.  On the other hand, Outview stands on it's own as a adventuresome work of fiction with appeal to all ages.

B007J4T2G8|4.0|First of all, remember... you are not reading Faulkner or Proust here. So, don't expect a monumental literary work. I say that up front b/c there is a lot of negative comments about the caliber of the writing and the superficiality of the story. To which I would like to respond: \\"Duh!\\" This is commercial fiction meant to entertain and provide a means of escape. It SHOULD be relaxing and not too taxing on the cerebrum.<br /><br />The only reason I read this book was b/c of all the hype. I've read very little erotica and, although I totally get it's utility, I have found it pretty ridiculous. So, I was doubtful that I could stand a full-length novel of the stuff.<br /><br />But, I would call this mainstream erotica or erotica for the non-erotica reader or, probably more apt, simply a romance novel with a little more hard core sex scenes. The sex scenes are probably a little too soft for the traditional erotica and a little too hard for the usual romance reader crowd.<br /><br />So... why the 4 stars when this isn't what I like to read? Because it was entertaining. I actually cared about what happened b/w Christian and Anastasia. I will actually buy the 2nd book. The characters weren't necessarily the most memorable and complex people you'd ever read about, but they were fleshed out enough that I wasn't bored. Also, there is a fairly long seduction process for the reader. Because of the hype, I already knew the relationship b/c Ana and Christian was supposed to be Dominant/Submissive with spankings etc. That's about all I knew. The author doesn't just fling you into that world. Like Ana, I guess we need some persuading. This is where, I think, James won over the masses. This is why this series is having mainstream success. You can believe that Ana would allow Christian to do what he does to her b/c she is taken there in a slow, step-wise fashion. And, like Ana, your excitement/anxiety/arousal about getting there builds through the book.<br /><br />What I don't like about the book? The same things I can't stand about the few romance novels I've read. Why does Christian have to be a millionaire? Are millionaires the only kind of men that can turn us on? Not only is he super rich... I don't think he is even 30 yet. Really? I'm rolling my eyes. And, of course, he's an accomplished pianist. Of course. And, of course, he's fluent in French. I'm getting nauseated. HOWEVER.... I let this slide b/c I think you can get past this cliche.<br /><br />Also, I echo the thoughts of another reviewer. Ana orgasms WAAAAYYY to easily. She was a virgin before Mr. Grey. She never even masturbated. This girl had never pleasured herself nor had an intimate relationship with a man to bring her close to orgasm. Yet, she orgasm with her first penetrative sexual experience in the capable hands of Christian Grey. I think the author could've been a little more realistic here and still made Mr. Grey out to be a sexual Jedi. But... like the multi-millionaire status thing... I'm letting it slide. I would've considered a 5 star, though, if the author made Christian middle class and made him work a little harder to make Ana cum. It would've made the story more tangible.<br /><br />Overall, this a good book that serves the purpose of entertaining the reader. Also, I think it might make you at least think about some of these sexual... how should I say this... accoutrements. Why not? I've been married for almost 18 years and, after reading this book, I had to ask my husband: \\"Do you ever think about spanking me?\\" LOL.<br /><br />Also, I have to say \\"Way to go!\\" to E L James. Good for you, girl. This book/series started off as an e book and then went to print-on-demand paperbacks. I love hearing those kind of success stories.

B00EQ30RVY|5.0|From the first listen all the way through, I was hooked.  I love this EP and give the Deleos, Eric, and Chester a huge thumb up for pulling off a most difficult task: making a real and true STP record without Scott Weiland.<br /><br />And they did.  After multiple listens today, I'm still digging High Rise, even when listening to earlier STP right after another spin through the new one.  It stands up solidly. Haters gonna hate, but they're without cause--this is good stuff!<br /><br />The songs have all the elements and heart that make STP great and great STP.  To whit:<br /><br />Out of Time:  A hard-charging, balls-to-the-wall opener in the vein of classic, early STP. Great way to start off a record--no easing in, just hard and fast and getting right to it.<br /><br />Black Heart: Infectious and and upbeat, a classic STP rocker. Hooky and, for me, one of the standout tracks, complete with a soaring bridge and yeah yeah yeahs you can't help but join in with.<br /><br />Same on the Inside: Reminds me more of Army of Anyone than earlier STP (and that's a compliment, since I think AoA is, overall, a more consistent and solid record than most in the STP discography (in terms of solidly standout songs).  Pretty and a little sad.  Good stuff.<br /><br />Cry Cry:  Another hooky, rollicking song that's instantly classic and very recognizable--in a good way!<br /><br />Tomorrow:  More mellow and downbeat, with those signature Dean riffs (not just the heir to Jimmy page riffmastery, but a skillful and innovative player in his own right!), an in-the-pocket rhythm section, and soaring, soulful vocals.  A great pick to close the EP out.<br /><br />Chester's voice is definitely reminiscent (if lighter-textured) than Scott's voice, and it's pretty clear they found someone who would fit neatly into the trademark STP sound. That said, this record is not \\"faking\\" being an STP record--it's as good as most and better than some (I'm looking at you, 2010 eponymous titled release with your two or three standout tracks)!  Don't get me wrong, I think Weiland is hugely talented and would never downplay his contribution to the band's image and sound (although I think Robert is definitely the bandleader and creative force in much of their output)--but I just don't miss him on this record!  Chester handles his lead singer duties beautifully! B005LAIIN0|3.0|Note: May contain spoilers. Proceed with caution.<br /><br />Over the past couple of years the zombie apocalypse genre has really exploded in almost every form of media. While I'll admit that the genre never really resonated with me like it did with others, I still found myself drawn to World War Z. Not because it looked different from the other films, video games, and novels out there and not because I'm a huge fan of Brad Pitt. It just looked... more humane than everything else I've seen up to this point. However, when it was all said and done, I can't say I enjoyed it as much as I hoped.<br /><br />Based on the 2006 novel of the same name, World War Z follows former United Nations employee Gerry Lane (Pitt). After being attacked by a hoard of zombies while sitting in traffic with his wife and kids, Gerry and his family find themselves on the run until a helicopter comes to pick them up and bring them to a U.S. Navy vessel. After his friend Thierry (Fana Mokoena) explains the situation, Gerry is forced to travel half-way across the world in order to find a cure for the virus. If not, he and his family are kicked off the vessel and left to fend for themselves.<br /><br />One of World War Z's strongest attributes is its inherent ability to keep you on the edge of your seat, literally. I usually watch movies laying down, but within the first fifteen minutes I found myself propped up in a seated position and I didn't move for the remainder of the movie. From start to finish, World War Z is an intense experience unlike any other. Whether Gerry is on the run from a massive group of zombies or creeping quietly through a zombie-infested building, your heart is bound to be pumping out of your chest. What makes it even more exciting is the fact that there's very little down time so this intensity is almost non-stop.<br /><br />Also, the CGI for this film is breathtakingly beautiful. The animation for the zombies is flawless and, at times, pretty insane. When you see these large flocks of zombies sprinting around, toppling over each other, or piling to reach the top of a gigantic wall, it's almost surreal. While I don't agree with the media's decision to constantly make these creatures sprint at 20+ mph, I can appreciate the level of detail that went into making them come to life. There's no doubt in my mind that it took a lot of time and dedication to make them perfect and it shows in the end.<br /><br />Unfortunately, that's where most of my enjoyment stopped. I was pulled in because of these extreme situations, but the narrative surrounding these moments is nothing short of mediocre. I never felt attached to Gerry and I never cared about his well-being or that of his family. They were disposable bags of flesh in an unfortunate circumstance and I never felt like I ever gained an understanding of Gerry as a human being. The only character I truly held any attachment to was a young soldier by the name of Segen (Daniella Kertesz). She may have only had a few lines of dialogue, but she was the only one who actually felt like more than just a prop in the story.<br /><br />Another thing that genuinely bothered me was the lack of closure. The conclusion to the story is so open-ended that it left me begging the question, what was the point? We followed Gerry through attacks, chases, plane crashes, and the like, but for what? So he could find a \\"camouflage\\"? That's it? Really? It made the entire journey essentially meaningless. I understand that not every story needs a happy or perfect ending, but at least arrive at a point. There needs to be some meaning behind everything that happened. Otherwise it winds up just being wasted time.<br /><br />With that said, though, Brad Pitt worked really well with the material he was given. His performance wasn't jaw dropping and it's not going to go down as one of the best performances of his career, but he did well. He was able to bring some life to an otherwise two-dimensional character and I commend him for that. I also have to give Kertesz her recognition as well. As I said earlier, she doesn't have much dialogue, but it's her mannerisms that really stand out and show her ability as an actress. I can't comment too much on anyone else because a great majority of the acting came from Pitt. Everyone else just had a few minutes here and there.<br /><br />Finally, the score, composed Marco Beltrami was solid. Some of the compositions were a little too generic for my taste, but they worked well within the film itself. They were loud and robust when they needed to be, but also quiet and eerie in just the right places. Beltrami definitely had a good understanding of what needed to be present here and he delivered with the perfect score. While it may not be one of the most impressive I've heard this year, it fits for the source material.<br /><br />Overall Score: 6.75/10 - While I did find myself intrigued during certain areas of World War Z, I can't say that I was overly impressed with the end result. It aims to be a serious entry into a genre that usually fails to be anything more than bizarre, but in its efforts it lost any sort of life that could have made it a fantastic film. I would only recommend this to the hardcore zombie enthusiasts. If you're a casual like me then I would say skip it or give it a rental.

(I will touch on plot points, but try to avoid detailed spoilers in the rest of the review).<br /><br />I absolutely adored the first 25% of the book. Margo and Q's interactions were fun, and the writing and plot kept me reading and trying to figure out where this crazy adventure was going. Margo was the kind of friend I wanted - who just... knew how to LIVE. Or so it seemed.<br /><br />But then Margo is pretty much absent for the other 75% of the book - during which Q tries to find her through understanding her. And while Q and his friends made for interesting dynamics, with both serious and entertaining moments... it didn't measure up to the first part of the book for me. The first quarter of the book propelled me through the last three-quarters, because I wanted to get back to Margo. I wanted to understand, solve the mystery, see the puzzle put together. Which, I suppose was the point, and what Q was feeling, thus what I was supposed to be feeling, but I still found that a little disappointing. I really prefer books where the journey to solve the mystery is as enjoyable as actually solving it, and in that aspect I, personally, was disappointed.<br /><br />The characters were mostly believable, and developed well (and appropriately to their characters) over the course of the book. Though I felt like Margo was a bit... beyond the typical high-schooler, I suppose it's not out of the realm of possibility. And I think the ending was appropriate to the characters.<br /><br />If you're looking for a light, easy, and entertaining escape from real life where everything's neatly wrapped up and everyone gets their happily ever after and you just come away with warm fuzzy happy feelings... this isn't the book for you. If you're looking for a book that reflects real life, and will take you on a journey of critiquing how you see the other people in your life... this is a good read.<br /><br />And as a side note, I do think it speaks to John Green's talent that he's able to explore deep topics of human existence and interaction in the moderately lighthearted ways that he does. I think I'll be reading more of his stuff.

B00BPDR3F6|5.0|It's obvious that Elizabeth Gilbert best known for her \\"first eat good food, then find a good guru, then find a good boyfriend,\\" book of recovery is going in a different direction with her latest. The Signature of All Things is a long, astonishingly researched historical about extreme individuals who do extreme things against a backdrop of wealth and - botany!  Think moss.  I don't mean to sound snarky because this epic novel took talent and hard work and a huge leap of faith to produce.  Some of the language is exquisite:  \\"She felt a deep bruise of absence for her father.\\" is a sentence that could only come from a writer that is giving us her best.  First I thought well, Elizabeth is writing a fabulous story of deprivation and wealth and plant life as a sound and grand metaphor for life.  If I'm smart enough, I'll get it.<br /><br />A serious look into 1800's Philadelphia grandeur is not what I was expecting from Gilbert.  Is this good or bad?  I don't know.  The beginning of this book is a quick set-up of the type that Jeffrey Archer did in Kane and Abel - very poor boy gets a foothold into a life that catapults him through happenstance, suffering and cunning into untold riches and power. That's okay with me.  I like a story that moves along as long as I care about the eventual outcome.  Once fame and fortune are accomplished, the storytelling slows down to introduce the real main character, Alma, a gifted brainiac, raised by the tiger mother of all tiger mothers. This tiger permits no frivolities, not even unkind thoughts. She instills an ethic of hard work that defines her daughter's life.  At times it consumes her life.<br /><br />Is it a love story?  Yes, but not in any ordinary sense.  Gilbert is going for transcendence.  Transcendence but still on earth - now it is clear why we have entered a religious order where plants lead us to the altar of the most high. And about that moss - Alma got me to fall in love with moss and Moss Time.<br /><br />I had the benefit of reading a lengthy profile of the author in the New York Times Magazine that spoke of Gilbert's process to settle on this project and her research for it.  It did not prepare me for Alma, the main character. Alma's emotions and ambitions and rigidity are not usual in popular fiction. Yet the outcome of her arduous journey to self-knowledge rings true and satisfies.<br /><br />Is this a compelling book - one that you must read to the finish?  Yes.  To the finish.efit of reading a lengthy profile of the author in the New York Times Magazine that spoke of Gilbert's process to settle on this project and her research for it.  It did not prepare me for Alma, the main character. Alma's emotions and ambitions and rigidity are not usual in popular fiction.
